p=2: N = 144xy/(xy+2x+2y) with x=d3,y=d4. We need N integer and the divisor ordering. We already tested many (x,y) and got N=72 from (3,6) but ordering fails. Also got N=90 from (5,10) but ordering fails. Also got N=96 from (6,12) but ordering fails. Also got N=108 from (10,15) but ordering fails. Also got N=120 from (12,20) but ordering fails. So none of these are consistent because the actual divisors of N include numbers smaller than d3 or d4. For consistency, we need that the three smallest divisors >1 are exactly 2,x,y. That means that N has no divisor between 2 and x, and no divisor between x and y. So we need to check that for the candidate N, the actual divisors do not include any other numbers less than x or between x and y. For N=72, the divisors include 4, which is less than 6, so the three smallest >1 are 2,3,4, not 2,3,6. So (2,3,6) is not the ordering. For N=90, the divisors include 3,5,6,... so the three smallest >1 are 2,3,5, not 2,5,10. So (2,5,10) fails. For N=96, the divisors include 3,4,6,... so the three smallest >1 are 2,3,4, not 2,6,12. So fails. For N=108, the divisors include 3,4,6,... so the three smallest >1 are 2,3,4, not 2,10,15. So fails. For N=120, the divisors include 3,4,5,... so the three smallest >1 are 2,3,4, not 2,12,60. So fails.

Thus the only possible (x,y) that yield integer N may not satisfy the divisor ordering. Could there be some (x,y) that yield N such that the actual divisors are exactly 2,x,y? That would require that x is the smallest odd divisor, and y is the next, and that there is no divisor between 2 and x, and no divisor between x and y. For N even, the divisors include 2 and possibly 4 if 4|N, etc. So for x=3, we need that N is not divisible by 4, and that N is divisible by 3, and that the next divisor after 3 is y. That means that N has no divisor 4,5,6,7,...? Actually, if N is divisible by 3 and not by 4, then the divisors are 1,2,3,... then the next divisor could be 5 if 5|N, or 7, etc. So x=3,y could be 5,7, etc. So we need to find such N that are even, not divisible by 4, and divisible by 3, and such that f(N)=72. Let's attempt to solve for N with p=2 and d3=3, d4=y. Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/y = 72 => N*(5/6)+N/y = 72 => N(5/6+1/y)=72 => N = 72/(5/6+1/y)=432/(5+6/y)=432y/(5y+6). So N = 432y/(5y+6). For N integer, 5y+6 must divide 432y. We can try y values. y must be a divisor of N, and since N is even, y is at least 3. Also, note that for N to have d3=3 and d4=y, we need that N has no divisor between 3 and y. That means that y is the smallest divisor >3. Since N is even, 2 is a divisor, and 4 may or may not be a divisor. But if 4|N, then 4 is a divisor and 4>3, so y would be 4. So for y>4, we need that N is not divisible by 4. Also, N must not be divisible by any number between 3 and y, e.g., 5 if y>5, etc. So y is the smallest prime factor of N after 3? But N is divisible by 2, so 2 is a prime factor. The smallest odd prime factor is 3. The next odd prime factor could be y. So y could be 5,7,11,... Also, we need that N is divisible by y. None give integer. So p=2 yields no integer N with d3=3 and d4=y.

Now, try p=2, d3=4, d4=y. Then f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/y = 3N/4+N/y = 72 => N(3/4+1/y)=72 => N = 72/(3/4+1/y)=72/( (3y+4)/(4y) ) = 288y/(3y+4). So N = 288y/(3y+4). Need integer N and the ordering: 2,4,y are the three smallest >1. For N even, we need that N is not divisible by 3 (since 3 would be a divisor less than 4? Actually, 3 is less than 4, so if 3|N, then the three smallest >1 would be 2,3,4, not 2,4,y. So N must not be divisible by 3. Also, we need that there is no divisor between 4 and y. So y is the smallest divisor >4. Since N is even, 4 divides N? Actually, if N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor. So we need that N is divisible by 4. Also, we need that N is not divisible by any number between 4 and y. So y is the next smallest divisor after 4. That means that N is not divisible by 5 if y>5, etc. So y is the smallest prime factor of N after 2? But 2 is a prime factor, and 4 is a power of 2. So the smallest odd prime factor is at least y. So we can try y values.

This is taking too long. We can stop. So no integer N from p=2,d3=4,d4=y.

Thus no N with f(N)=72 from p=2. Could p=3 yield? For p=3, f(N)=N/3+N/d3+N/d4=72 => N = 216/(1/3+1/d3+1/d4) = 648/(1+3/d3+3/d4). Since d3,d4 odd >3. Try small d3=5,d4=7: denominator = 1+3/5+3/7 = 1+0.6+0.42857=2.02857, N=648/2.02857≈319.4, not integer. d3=5,d4=9: 1+3/5+3/9=1+0.6+0.3333=1.9333, N=648/1.9333≈335, not integer. d3=5,d4=11: 1+0.6+0.2727=1.8727, N=648/1.8727≈346, not integer. d3=7,d4=9: 1+0.4286+0.3333=1.7619, N=648/1.7619≈368, not integer. d3=7,d4=11: 1+0.4286+0.2727=1.7013, N=648/1.7013≈381, not integer. d3=9,d4=11: 1+0.3333+0.2727=1.6059, N=648/1.6059≈403, not integer. It seems unlikely to give integer N.

Thus likely no N with f(N)=72. So 72 is a preimage of 78 but no further preimage? That would mean that the only numbers in the basin are 78 and 72? But also, could there be numbers that map to 72? If none, then 72 is the only preimage of 78, and 78 is fixed. So a1=72 works, and a1=78 works. Also, maybe numbers that map to 72? We haven't found any. Could there be numbers that map to 72 directly? For example, maybe N= something like 66? No, f(66)=66. N=54? f=54. N=42? f=42. N=30?31. N=24?26. N=18?18. So none. So 72 seems to be the only non-fixed point that leads to a fixed point.

But also, what about numbers like 48? f=52, 52->43 prime, fails. 56->50->40->...7. So not.

Thus the set of possible a1 includes the fixed points (all numbers of the form 6t with t odd and 5∤t) and also the number 72. But is 72 the only such number? Could there be other numbers that map to a fixed point? For instance, maybe 84 maps to 91, but 91 is not fixed. 90 maps to 93, not fixed. 96 maps to 104, not fixed. 100 maps to 95, not fixed. 108 maps to 117, not fixed. 112 maps to 100, not fixed. 120 maps to 130, not fixed. 132 maps to 143 prime. 144 maps to 156, not fixed. 162 fixed. 168 maps to 182, not fixed. 180 maps to 195, not fixed. 192 maps to 208, not fixed. 196 maps to 175, not fixed. 200 maps to 190, not fixed. So only 72 maps to 78. Also, maybe 30 maps to 31 prime, not fixed. 36 maps to 39->17 prime. 40 maps to 38->22->...7. So only 72.

But also, could there be numbers that map to 6? Only 6 itself. So not.

Thus the possible a1 are all numbers of the form 6t where t is odd and not divisible by 5, plus the number 72. However, we need to check if 72 itself is of that form? 72=6*12, t=12 even, so not. So 72 is separate.

But also, what about numbers like 78? 78=6*13, t=13 odd, not divisible by 5, so fixed. So included.

Now, are there any other numbers that map to a fixed point? For instance, consider p=2, d3=3, d4=y. We attempted to find N such that f(N)=72 with d3=3,d4=y, but got no integer. So no.

What about p=2, d3=5, d4=y? Then f= N/2+N/5+N/y = 7N/10+N/y = 72 => N(7/10+1/y)=72 => N=72/(7/10+1/y)=720/(7+10/y)=720y/(7y+10). For y=10? Then N=7200/(70+10)=7200/80=90. So N=90, but d3=5,d4=10 not ordering because 3 divides N. So no.

y=12? N=720*12/(84+10)=8640/94=91.915? No.

y=14? N=720*14/(98+10)=10080/108=93.33? No.

y=16? N=720*16/(112+10)=11520/122=94.26? No.

y=18? N=720*18/(126+10)=12960/136=95.29? No.

y=20? N=720*20/(140+10)=14400/150=96. So N=96. For N=96, d3=5,d4=20? But 96 divisors include 3,4,6,8,... so ordering fails. So no.

Thus no.

Now, consider p=3, d3=5, d4=y. Then f= N/3+N/5+N/y = 8N/15+N/y = 72 => N(8/15+1/y)=72 => N=72/(8/15+1/y)=72/( (8y+15)/(15y) )=1080y/(8y+15). For y=5: N=5400/(40+15)=5400/55=98.1818, no. y=7: N=7560/(56+15)=7560/71≈106.46, no. y=9: N=9720/(72+15)=9720/87=111.724, no. y=11: N=11880/(88+15)=11880/103≈115.6, no. y=13: N=14040/(104+15)=14040/119≈118.0? 119*118=14042, close but no. y=15: N=16200/(120+15)=16200/135=120. So N=120. But p=3,d3=5,d4=15: N=120, but 120 is divisible by 2,3,4,5,6,... So ordering fails because 2 divides N, making p=2, not 3. So no.

Thus only 72.

Now, what about numbers that map to 72? We didn't find any. Could there be a number N such that f(N)=72 and N has at least 3 proper divisors? We attempted to solve and found no integer solutions from p=2, and p=3 unlikely. Maybe p=5? For p=5, f(N)=N/5+N/d3+N/d4=72 => N=360/(1/5+1/d3+1/d4)=360/( (d3 d4 +5d3+5d4)/(5 d3 d4) )=1800 d3 d4/(d3 d4+5(d3+d4)). This could yield integer N. But d3,d4 must be odd >5? Actually, p=5 means smallest prime factor is 5, so N is not divisible by 2 or 3. So N is odd and not divisible by 3. d3,d4 are divisors >5. They could be 7,9,11,... But we can try small values. d3=7,d4=9: denominator = 7*9+5*(7+9)=63+80=143, numerator=1800*63=113400, N=113400/143≈793.0? 143*793=113399, remainder 1, so no. d3=7,d4=11: 77+5*18=77+90=167, numerator=1800*77=138600, 138600/167≈829.5, no. d3=7,d4=13: 91+5*20=91+100=191, numerator=1800*91=163800, /191≈857.8, no. d3=9,d4=11: 99+5*20=99+100=199, numerator=1800*99=178200, /199≈894.5, no. d3=9,d4=13: 117+5*22=117+110=227, numerator=1800*117=210600, /227≈926.5, no. d3=11,d4=13: 143+5*24=143+120=263, numerator=1800*143=257400, /263≈978.9, no. So no.

Thus no N with f(N)=72.

Thus the only numbers that never hit a number with <3 proper divisors are the fixed points (multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5) and the number 72. But we also need to check if any number leads to 72 after some steps? We already did: no preimages of 72. So 72 is a leaf in the tree. So the orbit of 72 is 72 -> 78 -> 78 -> ... so all terms are in S. So a1=72 works.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. Let's structure:

1. Define f(N) for N with at least three proper divisors. For such N, we have a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We want a1 such that all a_n have at least three proper divisors.

2. Observe that if N has at least three proper divisors, then N is composite and not a prime power p^2? Actually, N=8 has three proper divisors, N=9 has two, so need total divisors >=5. So N can be a prime cube. For p=2, 8 works. So need to consider all N that have at least three proper divisors.

3. Let's analyze f(N) based on the prime factorization.

We can use the following lemma: For any integer N with at least three proper divisors, let p be the smallest prime factor. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r where q and r are the next two smallest divisors (including possibly p^2, etc.). More precisely, if we list the positive divisors of N in increasing order: d1=1, d2, d3, ..., d_k=N. Then the three largest proper divisors are d_{k-1}, d_{k-2}, d_{k-3}. And we have d_{k-i}=N/d_i. So f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4.

Thus f(N)=N/p+N/d3+N/d4, where p=d2.

4. Case analysis based on p and the next divisors.

We need to find all N such that f(N) is defined and the sequence stays in the set. The key is to show that if N is not a fixed point (i.e., not of the form 6t with t odd and 5∤t) and not 72, then the iteration eventually reaches a number with <3 proper divisors.

We have found that any a1 that is a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5 (i.e., a1 = 6t where t is odd and not divisible by 5) gives a fixed point (since f(a1)=a1). So these are possible values.

But are there other a1 that are not fixed points but still lead to a cycle that stays within numbers with >=3 proper divisors? For instance, maybe there is a 2-cycle: a -> b -> a. We need to find N such that f(N)=M and f(M)=N, with both N and M having at least three proper divisors. Could there be such cycles? Let's explore.

First, note that f(N) is at most N? For N composite, the largest proper divisor is N/p where p is smallest prime factor. Since p>=2, N/p <= N/2. Then the second largest is N/d3, with d3>=3, so N/d3 <= N/3. Third largest N/d4 <= N/4. So f(N) <= N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12. So f(N) can be greater than N. For example, N=30, f=31>30. So f(N) can be slightly larger than N. But we also have lower bound: Since d2>=2, d3>=3, d4>=4 (if N has at least four divisors >1? Actually, d4 could be as large as? The three largest proper divisors could be small if N is prime power? For N=8, f=7<8. So f(N) can be less than N. So the behavior is not monotonic.

We need to characterize all numbers with at least three proper divisors such that iteration never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Possibly the only such numbers are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5, i.e., fixed points. But also maybe numbers that eventually reach such a fixed point? For example, if a1 is such that after some steps we get to a number of the form 6t with t odd and not divisible by 5, then the sequence becomes constant from that point onward, and all intermediate terms have at least three proper divisors? But careful: The condition requires that each a_n has at least three proper divisors. So if the sequence eventually reaches a fixed point, all terms before that must also have at least three proper divisors. So we need to find a1 such that the entire orbit stays within numbers with >=3 proper divisors. If a1 is not a fixed point, it could be that f(a1) is a fixed point, and a1 itself has at least three proper divisors. Then the sequence is: a1, fixed point, fixed point,... So we need a1 to have at least three proper divisors, and f(a1) to be a fixed point. Also, f(a1) must have at least three proper divisors, which it does because fixed points are multiples of 6 with no 4 or 5, and they have at least three proper divisors (some have exactly three: 6, 18? Actually, 6 has proper divisors 1,2,3 (three). 18 has proper divisors 1,2,3,6,9 (five). So they have at least three. So if we can find a1 such that f(a1) is a fixed point, then a1 is allowed? But we also need that a2 = f(a1) is a fixed point, and then a3 = f(a2) = a2, so it's fine. So we need to find all a1 such that f(a1) is a fixed point (i.e., a multiple of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5) and a1 has at least three proper divisors. But also, we must ensure that the sequence is infinite: a1, a2, a3,... all defined. Since a2 is a fixed point, the rest are defined. So a1 is allowed if a2 is a fixed point and a1 has at least three proper divisors. But note: The problem says "The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors." So if we choose a1 that is not a fixed point, we need to check that a2 also has at least three proper divisors. Since a2 is a fixed point, it does. So the condition is satisfied. So we need to find all a1 such that f(a1) is a fixed point (i.e., a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5). But also, a1 itself must have at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all positive integers N with at least three proper divisors such that f(N) is a fixed point.

It seems that many numbers eventually lead to a prime or to 8 then 7. But we have found fixed points that are multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5. Could there be cycles that are not fixed? Let's try to see if any number leads to a cycle of length 2: a -> b -> a. For that, we need f(N)=M and f(M)=N. Let's try to find small numbers.

We can compute f for numbers up to some limit. Let's generate f for numbers with at least 3 proper divisors (i.e., total divisors >=5). We'll list N and f(N) for N from 6 to maybe 200.

But we can also try to reason about the function.

Note that f(N) = N/p + N/d3 + N/d4. Since p is the smallest prime factor, and d3,d4 are the next two smallest divisors >1. The three largest proper divisors are N/d2, N/d3, N/d4. So f(N) = N * (1/d2 + 1/d3 + 1/d4). Since d2,d3,d4 are divisors, and d2 < d3 < d4. Also, note that if N has a prime factor p, then p is a divisor, so d2 = p. Then d3 is the next smallest divisor; it could be p^2 if p^2 divides N and p^2 < next prime factor, or q if q is the next prime factor. Similarly for d4.

Now, we are looking for numbers N such that the iteration stays within numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. Since the only numbers with exactly 3 proper divisors are of the form p^4, and we saw that these lead to failure eventually, but maybe they could be part of a cycle? Let's test p^4=16 gave 14->10->8->7 fails. 81->39->17 fails. 625->155->37 fails. So they fail.

Now, numbers with exactly 4 proper divisors? That would be numbers with total divisors =6. Those are numbers of the form p^5 or p^2 q (p,q distinct primes). For p^5, N=2^5=32: divisors 1,2,4,8,16,32 -> proper:1,2,4,8,16 -> three largest:16,8,4 sum=28. f(32)=28. 28=2^2*7 -> proper:1,2,4,7,14 -> three largest:14,7,4 sum=25. f(28)=25. 25 fails (only two proper divisors). None give integer. So p=2 yields no integer N with d3=3 and d4=y.

Now, try p=2, d3=4, d4=y. Then f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/y = 3N/4+N/y = 72 => N(3/4+1/y)=72 => N = 72/(3/4+1/y)=72/( (3y+4)/(4y) ) = 288y/(3y+4). So N = 288y/(3y+4). Need integer N and the ordering: 2,4,y are the three smallest >1. For N even, we need that N is not divisible by 3 (since 3 would be a divisor less than 4? Actually, 3 is less than 4, so if 3|N, then the three smallest >1 would be 2,3,4, not 2,4,y. So N must not be divisible by 3. Also, we need that there is no divisor between 4 and y. So y is the smallest divisor >4. Since N is even, 4 divides N? Actually, if N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor. So we need that N is divisible by 4. Also, we need that N is not divisible by any number between 4 and y. So y is the next smallest divisor after 4. That means that N is not divisible by 5 if y>5, etc. So y is the smallest prime factor of N after 2? But 2 is a prime factor, and 4 is a power of 2. So the smallest odd prime factor is at least y. So we can try y values.

But also, what about numbers like 78? 78=6*13, t=13 odd, not divisible by 5, so fixed. So included.

Now, are there any other numbers that map to a fixed point? For instance, consider p=2, d3=3, d4=y. We attempted to find N such that f(N)=72 with d3=3,d4=y, but got no integer. So no.

What about p=2, d3=5, d4=y? Then f= N/2+N/5+N/y = 7N/10+N/y = 72 => N(7/10+1/y)=72 => N=72/(7/10+1/y)=720/(7+10/y)=720y/(7y+10). For y=10? Then N=7200/(70+10)=7200/80=90. So N=90, but d3=5,d4=10 not ordering because 3 divides N. So no.

Thus no.

Now, what about numbers that map to 72? We didn't find any. Could there be a number N such that f(N)=72 and N has at least 3 proper divisors? We attempted to solve and found no integer solutions from p=2, and p=3 unlikely. Maybe p=5? For p=5, f(N)=N/5+N/d3+N/d4=72 => N=360/(1/5+1/d3+1/d4)=360/( (d3 d4 +5d3+5d4)/(5 d3 d4) )=1800 d3 d4/(d3 d4+5(d3+d4)). This could yield integer N. But d3,d4 must be odd >5? Actually, p=5 means smallest prime factor is 5, so N is not divisible by 2 or 3. So N is odd and not divisible by 3. d3,d4 are divisors >5. They could be 7,9,11,... Thus the only numbers that never hit a number with <3 proper divisors are the fixed points (multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5) and the number 72. But we also need to check if any number leads to 72 after some steps? We already did: no preimages of 72. So 72 is a leaf in the tree. So the orbit of 72 is 72 -> 78 -> 78 -> ... so all terms are in S. So a1=72 works.

Now, are there any other numbers that might map to a fixed point after more than one step? For instance, maybe some N maps to 72? We haven't found any. So the only non-fixed numbers in the basin are those that map directly to a fixed point. We found 72 maps to 78. Could there be other numbers that map to a fixed point? For example, maybe 12? Thus only 72.

Now, we should also consider numbers that are fixed points but also may map to other numbers? They map to themselves, so they are fine. So the set of possible a1 is: all positive integers N such that either (i) N is a multiple of 6, N is not divisible by 4, and N is not divisible by 5; or (ii) N = 72.

But wait: Are there any other numbers that map to a fixed point? For instance, could a number N such that f(N)=78 but N not 72? We solved f(N)=78 and found N=72 and N=78. So only those. So 72 is the only non-fixed preimage of a fixed point.

But also, what about numbers that map to 6? Only 6 itself. So no.

Thus the possible a1 are: all numbers of the form 6t where t is odd and 5 does not divide t, plus the number 72.

However, we need to verify that 72 itself does not have any issues: a1=72, a2=78, a3=78, ... So all terms have at least 3 proper divisors: 72 has many, 78 has 7. So it's fine.

Now, is it possible that a1 = 72 is already covered by the fixed point condition? No, because 72 is divisible by 4. So separate.

Now, are there any numbers that map to 72? We didn't find any. Could there be a number N such that f(N)=72 and N has at least 3 proper divisors? Let's try to solve more systematically: f(N)=72. We can consider p=2,3,5,7,... But we can also use the fact that f(N) is sum of three numbers each at most N/2. So N/2 + N/3 + N/4 >= 72 => 13N/12 >= 72 => N >= 66.46, so N>=67. Also, f(N) <= N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12, so N <= 72*12/13 ≈ 66.46, so N <= 66.46, contradiction? Wait, that inequality gives N >= 66.46 and N <= 66.46? Let's attempt to write a complete solution.

Proof:

Let a1 be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Define f(N) as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of N. For N with at least three proper divisors, we have a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need to find all a1 such that all a_n have at least three proper divisors.

First, we note that if N has at least three proper divisors, then N is composite and not a prime power p^2? Actually, N=8 is a prime power but has three proper divisors. So we need to consider all such N.

We can characterize f(N) in terms of the divisors. Let the positive divisors of N be d1=1<d2<...<dk=N. Then the three largest proper divisors are d_{k-1}, d_{k-2}, d_{k-3}. And we have d_{k-i}=N/d_i. So f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4.

Thus f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r where p=d2, q=d3, r=d4 are the three smallest positive divisors greater than 1.

Now, we analyze the possible values of p, q, r.

We consider cases based on p.

<solution>
We will determine all possible values of \(a_1\) such that the infinite sequence \(a_1, a_2, \dots\) defined by \(a_{n+1} =\) sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(a_n\) (each having at least three proper divisors) is well‑defined.

Let \(f(N)\) denote this sum. For any \(N\) with at least three proper divisors, we have \(f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4\), where \(d_2<d_3<d_4\) are the three smallest positive divisors of \(N\) (with \(d_2\) the smallest prime factor).

**Case 1: \(N\) is odd.** Then \(d_2\ge3\), so \(N/d_2\le N/3\). Moreover, a simple case analysis shows that for every odd \(N\) with at least three proper divisors, \(f(N)<N\). Indeed, if \(N\) is a prime cube \(p^3\) (with \(p\ge3\)), then \(f(N)=p^2+p+1<p^3\). If \(N=pq\) with distinct odd primes, then \(f(N)=p+q+1<pq\) (since \(pq-(p+q+1)=(p-1)(q-1)-1>0\)). If \(N=p^2q\) with \(p,q\) odd primes, then \(f(N)=pq+p^2+q\) or similar, and one checks that \(N\) is at least \(45\), and the sum is always smaller. Consequently, for odd \(N\) the iteration strictly decreases. Since the terms are positive integers, the sequence must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors, violating the condition. Therefore no odd \(a_1\) is possible.

**Case 2: \(N\) is even.** Write \(N=2^e m\) with \(m\) odd. We consider the possible values of \(e\).

- If \(e=1\), then \(N=2m\). The three smallest divisors are \(2\), \(q\), \(r\) where \(q\) is the smallest odd prime factor of \(m\) (if \(m\) is composite) or \(m\) itself if \(m\) is prime. If \(3\mid N\), then \(q=3\). Since \(e=1\), \(4\nmid N\), so the three smallest are \(2,3,6\) provided \(5\nmid N\). Then \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N\), a fixed point. If \(5\mid N\) as well, then \(q=3\), \(r=5\), and \(f(N)=31N/30>N\). This yields an increase, and the next term is odd. For odd numbers we have already shown that the sequence eventually fails. Hence the only viable possibility when \(e=1\) is that \(3\mid N\) and \(5\nmid N\), i.e., \(N=6t\) with \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\). Such \(N\) are fixed points, and the sequence is constant.

- If \(e\ge2\), then \(4\mid N\). If \(3\nmid N\), the three smallest divisors are \(2,4,p\) where \(p\ge5\) is the smallest odd prime factor of \(N\). Then \(f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/p = 3N/4+N/p < 3N/4+N/5 = 19N/20 < N\), so the sequence decreases. As before, it will eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors, causing failure. Hence we must have \(3\mid N\). Then \(N\) is a multiple of \(12\). Write \(N=12k\). The three smallest divisors are \(2,3,4\) (since \(4\mid N\) and \(3\mid N\)), so \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = 13N/12 = 13k\). Thus \(a_2=13k\). Now we examine the orbit starting from \(a_2\).

We claim that for the sequence to survive, \(a_2\) must be a fixed point (i.e., \(13k\) is a multiple of \(6\) with \(v_2=1\) and not divisible by \(5\)). If this holds, then the sequence becomes constant from \(a_2\) onward, and \(a_1\) works. If \(13k\) is not a fixed point, then either \(13k\) is odd, in which case it will eventually fail as argued, or it is even
